THE QUEEN'S UNIVERSITY OF BELFAST DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE 
REPORT OF AUTOPSY 
Name: Ian Henry OGLE  Sex:  Male  Age: 45 yrs.  F .No: 91/19  
Date of Death: 2711 , January, 201 9.  CGH  
Date and Hour of Autopsy: 2911' January, 2019.  8.45 a.m.  

Place of Autopsy: The Northern Ireland Regional Forensic Mortuary 
CAUSE OF DEATH: 
I (a) STAB WOUND OF CHEST 
Cm ti1c: i11s1ruc1ions oi� ci1e 1...oroncr tor Nonhem Ireland, Ms. Suzan,ne Anderson, I, Peter J. Ingram, MB, BCh, FRCPath, DMJ(Path), registered medical practitioner, Assistant State Pathologist for No11hern Ireland and Honorary Clinical Lecturer in Forensic Medicine at Queen's University of Belfast, made a postmortem examination of the body of 
IAN HENRY OGLE aged 45 years 
at the Northern Ireland Regional Forensic Mortumy on Tuesday, 29th January, 2019. Present at the autopsy was Detective Inspector A. Wright, P.S.N.I. Ladas Drive. 
During the course of the autopsy photographs were taken al my direclion by A. Cooper, P.S.N.l. 
�Photography Branch. 



THE QUEEN'S UNIVERSITY OF BELFAST DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE 
Name: Ian Herny OGLE Sex: Male Age: 45 yrs. F .No: 91/19 
Date ofDeath: 27'" January, 2019. CGH 
Date and Hour of Autopsy: 29'" Januaiy, 2019. 8.45 a.m. 
Place of Autopsy: The Northern Ireland Regional Forensic Mortuary 
HISTORY: 
The following history is based 011 information supplied lo the pathologist, at the time of the autopsy, by the i11vestigati11g police officer andji-om other sources. The accuracy of the information co11tai11ed in the history has 110/ been verified. 
He was engaged and he lived with his fiancCe of many years in a Housing Executive terrace house. He was unemployed but previously had worked as a window fitter. He was diagnosed with both asthma and depression in 1997. I-le had suffered from abdominal pains between 2003 and 2006 which had been investigated but no cause was found. He was also diagnosed with alcohol dependence syndrome in 2009. In 2014 he had taken an overdose. He saw his doctor in September 2018 complaining of agitation, shorl temper and feeling stressed. He was currently being prescribed fluoxetinc, pregabalin, co-codamol and two inhalers. He was a non.smoker and his alcohol intake had moderated. 
Since about 2017 he had been under threat from loyalist paramilitaries on account ofhis son1s behaviour. At around 8.50 p.rn. on January 27, an employee in a chip shop on the Beersbridge Road telephoned the police informinP them there lrnrl hee11 ,1 fi!:_Jht n11tc:idt"' Thic: rn.n wci"' in flw "iciJ,;+:, .. !.:.;,: �.'.:.:.�: 1:-::. it i: ;:.;: ),:.::.;�;,�;_; i!'!:..:.:. was involved in the fight. At around 9.25 p.m. he was standing in the street not far from his home chatting with a local pastor. As the pastor spoke to him the pastor became aware of five men, who were masked, walking towards them from the direction ofTcmplcmorc Avenue. This man turned around and saw the masked men approaching. They then ran towards this man and assaulted him. During the course of the assault he fell to the ground where it is believed he was kicked. A baseball bat may also been used in the assault. He was also kicked in the head and his head may have been stamped upon. The five males then moved away, got into a car and drove off. A member of the public had seen one of the attackers holding a knife. The pastor telephoned the emergency services. A rapid response vehicle was first upon the scene. When the paramedic examined this man he found no evidence of a cardiac output but noted what he thought was two stab wounds in his back. He did not observe much blood loss al the scene although it was quite dark. Shortly thereafter an ambulance arrived and between the ambulance crews they instigated attempts at resuscitation. An i-gel tube was inserted and chest compressions were undertaken. These attempts were continued en route to hospital. Adrenaline was also administered nnd bilaternl finger thoracostomies were undertaken. 
On arrival at hospital the resuscitative efforts were ongoing. An anaesthetist removed the i-gel tube and sited an endotracheal tube. Intravenous lines were placed and an intraosseous needle was inserted to the right lower limb. Massive blood transfusion was undertaken using O negative blood. Formal bilateral chest drains were subsequently sited and air was found to be present in the left chest cavity. Further vials ofadrenaline were administerede�1. However it became apparent that these attempts at resuscitation were futile and following discussion with the medical team the decision was taken to abandon the resuscitative efforts. Life was formally pronounced extinct at 1e0.12 p.m. 
F. 91/19 
The body was received in a blue plastic body bag which had been secured with a security tag bearing the unique identifying sequence "Wl 9251472". It was opened in my presence. The head had been covered with a white cardboard box. 
EXTERNAL EXAMINATION: 
The heavily tattooed body of a middle-aged man of stout build, weighing I 03.0 kg. and measuring 173 cm. (5 ft. 8 inches) in length. Rigor mortis was present. Hypostasis of pink colour stained the back of the body. 
There were the following signs ofn1edical intervention:.
1. 
An endotracheal tube protmded from the right side of the mouth.

Two needle puncture marks in the fold of the right elbow and separated by 2 cm. of uninjured skin. 
4. 
An intravenous cannula had been inserted through the skin in the fold of"the lell elbow and was held in place by an adhesive dressing. 

5. 
An intrnosseous needle had been inserted through the skin of the upper right shin. 

6. 
A single lumen intravenous cannula had been inserted through the skin in the fold of the \en groin and was held in place by adhesive tape. 

7. 
A horizontal thorncostomy incision, 2.5 cm. long and gaping by up to 0.5 cm., in the right mid axillary line and centred approximately 4 cm. below the fold of the axilla. It had been covered by some elasticated tape, 


A chest drain had been inserted on the left side of the chest through a horizontal surgical incision, 2.4 cm. long. It was held in place by a surgical stitch and covered by elasticated dressings. 
9. 
A broad bore needle puncture mark on the right side of the front of the chest and centred I 0 cm. below and 7.5 cm. to the right of the suprastemal notch. 

10. 
A broad bore needle puncture mark on the left side of the front of the chest and centred 11 cm. below and 9 cm. to the left of the suprastcmal notch. 

11.

COMMENTARY: 
The histmy indicates that this middle-aged man was assaulted in a street by a number of males, one of whom was believed to have been holding a kni[e. During the course of the assaull he foll to the ground .. The attackers then made off and the emergency services were called. A rapid response vehicle was first to afrive and when the paramedic examined this man there were no signs of life. Attempts at resuscitation were:instigated ..d continued en route to hospital where further attempts at resuscitation were made including massive b_lood transfusion and the inse1iion of drains into both chest 'cavities but ultimately to no avail. 
This was the body of an adult male of stout build, weighing l 03.0 kg. (I 6 stone 2 lb) ai\d measl)ring 173 
'. '�e' cm. (5 feet 8 inches). He was heavily tattooed. 
.JO.

He had been stabbed a total of eleven times. Two of the wounds were on the back of the right upper am1. Another stab wound was situated on the right side of the back quite close to the back of the armpit and the other eight stab wounds were clustered in an area centred about the left side of the lop of the back. However only one of the eleven wounds had penetrated the body sufficiently deeply to have caused any life threatening internal injury. The fatal wound was situated on the left side of the upper back quite close to the midline. The blade of the knife had passed between the fifth and sixth ribs partially dividing the sixth rib close to the spine. It had nicked the sixth bone of the spine in the chest before partially transecting the wall of the aorta, the principal artery of the body, incising the lower lobe of the left lung, partially dividing the main bronchus of the left lower lobe, penetrating the sac of the heart and nicking the surface of the heart. Whilst the other wounds would have bled quite freely it was the catastrophic bleeding, or haemo1Thage, both internally and quite possibly externally, principally from the aortic injury which led to his rapid death. No more than moderate force would have been required to inflict any of the stab wounds, even the fatal wound, particularly if the blade of the weapon was sharp and pointed. 
A number of other injuries were also noted. There was a large area of abrasion on the left side of the outer aspect of the forehead set within which was a rather ragged laceration. The underlying scalp here was brnisecl and there was a fine fracture of the skull involving the very thin roof of the left eye socket. The underlying brain was uninjured. This surface injury and the fracture were almost ce1tainly as a result of his having collapsed onto a hard unyielding surface. It played no part in his death. There was also a discrete bruise on the right side of the front of the forehead as well as brnising of the pinna of the right e�ar with a number of associated abrasions on and around the car. A boggy bruise was also sustained on the right side of the scalp above the ear and there was some superimposed abrasion. Furthem10re these injuries were associated with some bruising over the underlying scalp. The bruise on the forehead could have been caused by a punch whereas those injuries noted on and around the car and on the right side of the scalp were more likely as a result of a kick or kicks from a shod foot. 
There were also two brnises on the inner aspect of the lower lip, each of which bore a superimposed laceration. Thc::;e injuries were cousislent with a punch or punches to the face. Some bruises un the inner aspecls of each upper arm may have occurred when he was being lifted and transported to hospital or gripped tighlly during the course of a fight. Other bruises on the arms and one on the back of the right hand, some of which were associated with small abrasions, could have been sustained as a result of his attempting to defend himself although one or two of these which were associated with abrasion could have occuffed when he collapsed onto the ground. .lj\.,rc 1, \.-l \_, cii,,,, 'wni;:,1..,:, Ull t:,\., :}<\\.,.:, ur i WU ui' lhi;;; .1Ul,h._Jc::, vi' tile kfl ha11U as w1..:ii as a oruise 011 rne bc.lCK 01 me first joint of the left ring finger. Bruises were also present on the back of the knuckles of the right middle, ring and little fingers as well as one close to the first joint of the middle finger where there was a superficial laceration. These injmies could have been sustained as a result of his having attempted to defend himself from blows but equally well they could have been as a result of his slriking someone or something prior to his death. There were also some bruises on the lower limbs but these did not appear to be recent. 
The autopsy also revealed severe degenerative narrowing of one of the coronary arteries of the heart. This is unlikely to have accelerated his death to any material extent as the fatal wound would have been almost invariably fatal even in a healthy individual. Therefore it does not warrant inclusion in the cause of death. There was also a slight grade of emphysema of the lungs but this was merely an incidental finding. 
The report of Alere Forensics shows that at the t'imc of his death there was some alcohol in the body. Its concentration in the bloodstream and urine, 128 and 179 rng/dL respectively, indicates that he was slightly intoxicated when he died. However it must be borne in mind that he was transfused blood whilst in hospital which may have caused a dilutional effect and the level of alccihol present in the bloodstream at the time of the attack could have been higher. That said the urine concentration detected would suggest that the blood level present at the time of the attack is unlikely to have been �significantly high,er. The blood was also analysed for a range of conunon drugs or abuse and prescription medicines. The antidepressant fluoxetine and the painkiller paracetamol were detected at therapeutic levels. He was being prescribed both of these drugs. The analysis also detected that the anti psychotic quetiapine and the benzodiazepine diazepam were present neither of which he was currently being prescribed. Propranolol, a drug used to treat a number of conditions including anxiety and hea1i 
-11 .

{ 
F.e9e1/l9 
disease, was also found to be in the blood. He was not currently being prescribed this medicine but had been in the past. None of the drugs contributed to the fatal outcome. 
His body was identified by the taking of his fingerprints and a comparative analysis with those held on police records.

Transcript re Body Worn footage from Constable Tohill on the 27/1/19 at 22:15.exhibit ST1. 
Start of the footage is from outside 3 Wolff Close (home of Jill Morrison and Jonathan Brown) 
ARV police arrive at 22:15 and await local police arrival. 
22:22:15 -local police rap the door at 3 Wolff Close. 
22:22:34 -Female answers the door and states that it is just her and her wee girl in the house. She consents to police entering the house. 
22:22:40 -Police officer asks the female if she is the owner of the black Seat Leon JGZ 7476 
Female replies yes and states that her name is Jill Morrison. 
22:23:07 -Police ask if anyone else has access to the vehicle, Morrison replies that Jonathan Brown also has access to the vehicle and that he occasionally stays with her but is resident in 19 McArthur Court. 
22:23:50 -Police explain to Morrison that witnesses have seen people go to or from that vehicle with a knife and asked her did she know anything about that. She replies no. 
22:24:06 -Police ask Morrison 1f slit: kno�;:s 1!.'erA Jonathan is at the moment and she replies no. 
22:24:31 -Police ask Morrison if she drives the car, she replies yes. She stated that she was out in it earlier on that night around 6ish. Morrison stated that she went to the Chinese. 
22:24:50 -Morrison is asked if Brown went out in the car after her, but she stated that she wasn't sure as her daughter had been unwell and she had been upstairs with her. She stated that Brown had keys to the car. 
22:26:01 -Police asked Morrison if she would be happy for them to check the house and she replied 'yeah go ahead'. 
22:28:32 
-Police start a search of the house for Jonathan with Morrison's consent. 

22:28:32 
-Police asked Morrison how long she had been in a relationship with Brown, she stated a few years. 





Second part of video -exhibit ST1 a 
22:31 
:35 -House search completed. Brown not at the address. 

22:31

Q.

A. 
He was yeah.

He's a tiler.

3.

are to be made above the line).

5.

6.

7.

8. 
You should also state in the statement of evidence how long you have known the suspect and how. 

9. 
Obviously it must be stressed that reference to previous arrests or convictions, etc should not be included in the statement of evidence as this would be deemed prejudicial at any subsequent proceedings.

INTRODUCTION 
1. 
I am a Senior Forensic Investigator at Diligence International, a group specialising in forensic video, imagery, audio, computers, mobile devices, questioned documents and cell site analysis. I am a founder member of the Forensic Imagery Analysis Group (now FIAD). I am listed in the UK Register of Expert Witnesses and was registered in Imaging with the CRFP. 

2. 
I hold a Bachelor of Science Degree, a BTEC HNC and ONC in Electrical and Electronic Engineering. I am a Fellow of the Institute of Engineering & Technology. I served in the Royal Navy for 25 years, undertaking a broad spectrum of Electronic Engineering work. The culmination of my naval career was as the Weapons Engineer Officer of a Nuclear Submarine. 

3. 
From 1999 to June 2008 I was employed by Control Risks Forensics as the senior video consultant, before establishing my own consultancy, Infra Tech Forensics. From April to December 2019 I was employed by BAE Systems before returning to the forensic profession. I specialise in providing expertise in the enhancement and authentication of video material and image analysis. I have successfully completed the Police Air Reconnaissance Imagery Analysis Course at the ,Joint School of 

4. 
Shortly after the 7 /7 London Bombings, I carried out the processing of Police & Security Services surveillance video material in support of the Court Presentation of OPERATION CREVICE (R v Omar Khyam & others.) I have provided expertise and support pertaining to the analysis, enhancement and authentication of video and audio material, image analysis and comparison of faces, clothing, vehicles and firearms. In addition I have carried out compilation editing, subject tracking, dynamic even\ analysis, person height analysis, object identification and video reconstructions. I have applied these skills in many criminal and some civil cases, for both the prosecution and defence. I have given evidence in the Royal Courts of Justice, the Central Criminal Court, the High Court of the Justiciary, Crown, Magistrates and Coroner's Courts. I have lectured to police detective sergeant training courses.

GENERAL COMMENT 
13. 
For Male 1, there are two distinct parts to this image analysis procedure; the bus shelter footage near the locus of the incident and the later footage from Harland Walk, Frazer Pass and Frame Street. 

14. 
Outside the Prince Albertsbridge Bar and in the bus shelter footage, the Police proposal is that Male 1 is wearing Male 2's top (as recorded the day before.) In the post incident footage, Male 1 is wearing a different clothing ensemble. 


Levels of Support 
15. The conclusions will normally be expressed using terminology endorsed by the Forensic Image Analysis Division (FIAD) detailed in Appendix A, as either: 
a. 
A statement that, on the bas�1s of irreconcilable differences, the two (2) subjects or objects being compared are not the same and are therefore EXCLUDED from further contention. 

b. 
A statement of the degree of support the analysis lends for the contention that the two (2) subjects or objects are the same, as outlined below: 


Lends NO SUPPORT Lends LIMITED SUPPORT Lends MODERATE SUPPORT Lends SUPPORT Lends STRONG SUPPORT Lends POWERFUL SUPPORT 
c. This scale of conclusion was devised by FIAD in response to requests from the Judiciary for standardisation of experts' opinions in Imagery Analysis evidence. [arlier concluding evidence had been known to cause confusion. Even where experts agreed on matters, their scales of support or use of terminology appeared to show differences of opinion. 
16. The widely adopted scale provides guidance to our findings with an easily interpreted level; but it should be noted that there are no defined thresholds between levels of 
DI LI GENC E 
�� \ ! �,�,I A:>\ 1,: 
Page 6 
19189v_PSN//MJB/1 
the scale and that, in instances where two (2) experts conclude with an adjacent level of support (rather than the same level), there will be much common ground in their findings. 'Polar opposites' between experts' conclusions will imply a degree of disagreement in their findings or interpretation. 
17. 
The absence of statistical analysis in imagery comparison exercises is such that percentages or numerical values cannot be used to express our findings. For example, 'Powerful Support' does not infer a one-hundred percent (100%) match. 

18. 
I reserve the right to vary the terminology when to do so would more accurately express my conclusions.

Appendix A -Guidance for Evaluating Levels of Support 
This guidance note is endorsed by the Forensic Imagery Analysis Division (FIAD) within the British Association of Human Identification (BAHID). The purpose of the note is to provide broad guidance to lawyers and others seeking to understand the use of Levels of Support, and, in general terms, how any stated conclusion may be justified. The note is non-binding on practitioners and it is the strong opinion of the group that it is the expert's professional duty to evaluate each case independently and draw conclusions based upon this guidance note, their own professional experience and any prevalence statistics or other evidence that is relevant to the case and contained within, or referred to, in their report. 
For each of the levels of support, it is not possible to indicate a specific number of required similarities as the factors of most importance differ from case to case. The levels of support should not be seen as a 'stepped' or mutually exclusive set of categories but rather a continuous spectrum whereby each level blends into the next and no distinct cut-off point can be defined. 
It is important to note that the comparisons undertaken do not require satisfying each of the criteria for each level of support. For example, in the case of 'Powerful Support' the images may be of very good quality, with a high level of observable detail but an assessment of relative geometry may not necessarily have been undertaken. 
It is also important to note that the Levels of Support can be used in order to express the weight of imagery evidence that two persons imaged are not one and the same. For example, it may be that differences can be seen between imagery of an unknown person and the suspect/defendant. These differences might not be sufficient to justify the elimination of the suspect, but may raise such concern thc1t a cond1e1sion of mnrlPr.tp support that the two persons are not one and the same is appropriate. 
This note is not definitive. It has been prepared to provide guidance on conclusions in cases of facial comparisons, although the Levels of Support are also widely used in other types of comparative interpretation. The following factors are taken into account when defining each level of the scale: 
� 
The image quality 

� 
The number of fadal feature similarities/differences observed 

� 
The number of different times the similarities/differences can be observed, 

� 
Whether or not a comparison of relative geometry can be made, and the closeness of any correlation or difference 

� 
The number of different viewpoints available 

� 
Individual significance attributed to a distinct correlation or difference of a given feature, or several features in combination 

� 
The presence/absence of marks, scars and blemishes 


DI LIGENCE 

Page 12 19189V_PSNI/MJB/ 1 
No support 
� 
The imagery is too poor to permit a sufficiently robust comparison from which to form an expert opinion. 

� 
There is sufficient detail from which to form an expert opinion, but the results are such that the similarities are countered by differences, making the imagery evidence uncertain and inconclusive. 

� 
There may be very general characteristics observable (i.e. race, gender, hair colour), but these features do not offer enough support towards specific identity, for an expert to make a more meaningful comparison than a lay person. It would be unsafe to offer any support towards the contention that the two persons under comparison are one and the same using facial image comparison methodology. 


Limited support 
� 
The image quality does not allow for the detail of features to be seen. 

� 
The imagery allows for detail to be seen, but obscuration (hood, hat, scarf etc.) prevents a comprehensive comparison. 

� 
There are a few general characteristics observable, these would include descriptors such as broad nose, moderately protrusive ears etc. Individually or in combination, these features only allow for a 'superficial' comparison. The ability to discriminate between two people of similar general appearance is restricted. 

� 
ThP (ne= .!8th,P! f.ri.I s.1enn:0t./* !::'.:.' CJ'.' me:.' r',oGt hC.'v'C bcGll CV11�1fJa.1euU, Uul uL.is.1eVdlior1 of identifiable landmarks is severely restricted by image quality. 

� 
Only one or two adequate images may be available for comparison. 


' An assessment of relative facial geometry requires that images of the crime perpetrator and the suspect are selected from the same viewpoints and scaled to be the same size. Alignment of the various landmarks (usually facial feature locations) observable, in each of the images, may reveal differences in the facial proportions or the s'1zes of the facial features between the two items of comparison. A comparison of facial geometry in this way is an assessment of the relative size, shape and position of the various facial features. Differences are more likely to be evident when the imagesare aligned on both horizontal and vertical axes rather than on one axis alone. 
DI LIGENCE 

Page 13 19189v_PSN!IMJBI 1 
( 
Moderate support 
� The image quality permits a moderate amount of facial feature detail to be discerned: 
i.e. visible features can be described; for example, the nose could be classified as having a narrow bridge and a straight ridge. 
� 
There are a few general facial features that can be compared. 

� 
Individually or in combination, the available imagery allows for a reasonably robust comparison. The ability to discriminate between two people of similar appearance is restricted. 

� 
The (relative) facial geometry may be matching, but the ability to precisely locate landmarks is restricted and rnay therefore be somewhat imprecise. 

� 
The comparison may be limited in terms of the number of images available. 

Support 

� 
The image quality permits individual features to be compared in detail and a confident interpretation and comparison can be made. 

� 
There are some facial features that can be compared in detail. It is likely that the facial similarities/differences will be evident on multiple images. However, they may also be observed on only one occasion depending on image quality. The similarities may or may not be obse,ved in combination. 


o Individually or in combin8tion thP ::::i,,.i!!'.=l_ble ir--n.9.:;;0;'/ :]!!:::':.1:::: fct c ;-v.u0l vu111�a1i;:-;u11. 
It should be possible to make a distinction between two people of broadly similar appearance. However, the ability to discrim'1nate between two people bearing a close resemblance is restricted. 
� 
The (relative) facial geometry may be matching, but the ability to precisely locate landmarks �1s restricted and may therefore be somewhat imprecise. An assessment of geometry is likely to be limited to one or two instances i.e. from one or two differing viewpoints. 

� 
The comparison would usually be based on multiple images to provide consistency and confidence of interpretation. 


DILIGENCE 
Page 14 
19189v_PSNI /MJB /1 
Strong support 
� 
The image quality permits a range of features to be compared in detail and a very confident interpretation and comparison can be made. 

� 
There are some facial features that can be compared in detail. It is likely that the facial similarities/differences will be evident on multiple images. However, they may also be observed on only one occasion depending on �1mage quality. The similarities may or may not be observed in combination. 

� 
Individually or in combination, the available imagery allows for a robust comparison. It should be possible to make a distinction between two people of similar appearance. However, the ability to discriminate between two people bearing a very close resemblance is restricted. 

� 
The (relative) facial geometry may be matching; it is possible to confidently locate landmarks. A confident assessment of geometry is likely to be available from multiple viewpoints. 

� 
The comparison would usually be based on multiple images showing good detail to provide consistency and confidence of interpretation. 

� 
A lesser strength of comparison may be augmented by similarities/differences in the location and size of tattoos, marks and scars, although fine detail of these features cannot be determined with confidence. 


Powerful support 
� 
The image quality permits a range of features to be compared in significant detail and an extremely confident interpretation and comparison can be made. 

� 
A significant number of facial features can be compared in detail. It is likely that these facial similarities/differences will be evident on multiple images. However, they may also be observed on only one occasion depending on image quality. The similarities may or may not be observed in combination. 

� 
Individually or in combination, the available imagery allows for a robust comparison. It should be possible to make a distinction between two people of very similar appearance. Two people WOllld have to bear a striking resemblance in order for discrimination not to be possible. 

� 
Unless clearlyjustified by other aspects of the comparison, the assessment of (relative) facial geometry should be very robust, based on several landmarks that can be located with great confidence. A confident assessment of geometry is likely to be available from multiple viewpoints [notwithstanding comparisons based on high quality photography such as passports]. 


DI LI G ENCE 
Page 15 
191891/_PSNJ/MIB/ 1 
� 
The comparison would usually be based on multiple images showing good detail to provide consistency and confidence of interpretation. 

� 
A lesser strength of comparison may be augmented by similarities/differences in the location and size of tattoos, marks and scars, with the ability to compare some detail with reasonable confidence [shape or general delineation]. 


This guidance note was accepted by the group at their meeting in 2 December 2008 and should be read in conjunction with the Association of Chief Police Officers (ACPO) Working Group for Facial Identification Manual (section 2 and appendix 3 in particular); and other guidance notes issued by FIAD

INTRODUCTION 
1. 
I am a Senior Forensic Investigator at Diligence International, a group specialising in forensic video, imagery, audio, computers, mobile devices, questioned documents and cell site analysis. I am a founder member of the Forensic Imagery Analysis Group (now FIAD). I am listed in the UK Register of Expert Witnesses and was registered in Imaging with the CRFP. 

2. 
I hold a Bachelor of Science Degree, a BTEC HNC and ONC in Electrical and Electronic Engineering. I am a Fellow of the Institute of Engineering & Technology. I served in the Royal Navy for 25 years, undertaking a broad spectrum of Electronic Engineering work The culmination of my naval career was as the Weapons Engineer O.icer of a Nuclear Submarine. 

3. 
From 1999 to ,June 2008 I was employed by Control Risks Forensics as the senior video consultant, before establishing my own consultancy, Infra Tech Forensics. From April to December 2019 I was employed by BAE Systems before returning to the forensic profession. I specialise in providing expertise in the enhancement and authentication of video material and image analysis. I have successfully completed the Police Air Reconnaissance Imagery Analysis Course at the Joint School of Photogmpl1ic Interpretation. 

4. 
Shortly after the 7 /7 London Bombings, I carried out the processing of Police & Security Services surveillance video material in support of the Court Presentation of OPERATION CREVICE (R v Omar Khyam & others.) I have provided expertise and support pertaining to the analysis, enhancement and authentication of video and audio material, image analysis and comparison of faces, clothing, vehicles and firearms. In addition I have carried out compilation editing, subject tracking, dynamic event analysis, person height analysis, object identification and video reconstructions. I have applied these skills in many criminal and some civil cases, for both the prosecution and defence. I have given evidence in the Royal Courts of Justice, the Central Criminal Court, the High Court of the Justiciary, Crown, Magistrates and Coroner's Courts. I have lectured to police detective sergeant training courses.

Levels of Support 
15. The conclusions will normally be expressed using terminology endorsed by the Forensic Image Analysis Division (FIAD) detailed in Appendix A, as either: 
a. 
A statement that, on the basis of irreconcilable differences, the two (2) subjects or objects being compared are not the same and are therefore EXCLUDED from further contention. 

b. 
A statement of the degree of support the analysis lends for the contention that the two (2) subjects or objects are the same, as outlined below: 


DI LIG ENCE 
.\,,)! 1, 1 �,� ',�, I \''''f�I 

Lends NO SUPPORT Lends LIMITED SUPPORT Lends MODERATE SUPPORT Lends SUPPORT Lends STRONG SUPPORT Lends POWERFUL SUPPORT 
c. This scale of conclusion was devised by FIAD in response to requests from the Judiciary for standardisation of experts' opinions in Imagery Analysis evidence. Earlier concluding evidence had been known to cause confusion. Even where 
experts agreed on matters, their scales of support or use of terminology 
appeared to show differences of opinion. 
16. 
The widely adopted scale provides guidance to our findings with an easily interpreted level; but it should be noted that there are no defined thresholds between levels of 

the scale and ttiat, in instances where two (2) experts conclude with an adjacent level of support (rather than the same level), there will be much common ground in their findings. 'Polar opposites' between experts' conclusions will imply a degree of disagreement in their findings or interpretation. 

17. 
The absence of statistical analysis in imagery comparison exercises is such that percentages or numerical values cannot be used to express our findings. For example, 'Powerful Supporte' does not infer a one-hundred percent (100%) match. 

18. 
I reserve the right to vary the terminology when to do so would more accurately express my conclusions.

Appen dix A-Guidance for Evaluating Levels of Support 
This guidance note is endorsed by the Forensic Imagery Analysis Division (FIAD) within the British Association of Human Identification (BAHIO). The purpose of the note is to provide broad guidance to lawyers and others seeking to understand the use of Levels of Supporte, and, in general terms, how any stated conclusion may be justified. The note is non-binding on practitioners and it is the strong opinion of the group that it is the expert's professional duty to evaluate each case independently and draw conclusions based upon this guidance note, their own professional experience and any prevalence statistics or other evidence that is relevant to the case and contained within, or referred to, in their report. 
For each of the levels of support, it is not possible to indicate a specific number of required similarities as the factors of most importance differ from case to case. The levels of support should not be seen as a 'stepped' or mutually exclusive set of categories but rather a continuous spectrum whereby each level blends into the next and no distinct cut-off point can be defined. 
It is important to note that the comparisons undertaken do not require satisfying each of the criteria for each level of support. For example, in the case of 'Powerful Supporte' the images may be of very good quality, with a high level of observable detail but an assessment of relative geometry may not necessarily have been undertaken. 
It is also important to note that the Levels of Support can be used in order to express the weight of imagery evidence that two persons imaged are not one and the same. For example, it may be that differences can be seen between imagery of an unknown person and the suspect/defendant. These differences might not be sufficient to justify the eliminc1tion of the suspect, but may raise such concern that a conclusion of moderate support that the two persons are not one and the same is appropriate. 
This note is not definitive. It has been prepared to provide guidance on conclusions in cases of facial comparisons, although the Levels of Support are also widely used in other types of comparative interpretation. The following factors are taken into account when defining each level of the scale: 
� 
The image quality 

� 
The number of facial feature similarities/differences observed 

� 
The number of different times the similarities/differences can be observed, 

� 
Whether or not a comparison of relative geometry can be made, and the closeness of any correlation or difference 

� 
The number of different viewpoints available 

� 
Individual significance attributed to a distinct correlation or difference of a given feature, or several features in combination 

� 
The presence/absence of marks, scars and blemishes 




DILIGENC E 


Page 13 19189V_aPSN!aIMJaBa/a2 

No support 
� 
The imagery is too poor to permit a sufficiently robust comparison from which to form an expert opinion. 

� 
There is sufficient detail from which to form an expert opinion, but the results are such that the similarities are countered by differences, making the imagery evidence uncertain and inconclusive. 

� 
There may be very general characteristics observable (i.e. race, gender, hair colour), but these features do not offer enough support towards specific identity, for an expert to make a more meaningful comparison than a lay person. It would be unsafe to offer any support towards the contention that the two persons under comparison are one and the same using facial image comparison methodology. 


Limited support 
� 
The image quality does not allow for the detail of features to be seen. 

� 
The imagery allows for detail to be seen, but obscuration (hood, hat, scarf etc.) prevents a comprehensive comparison. 

� 
There are a few general characteristics observable, these would include descriptors such as broad nose, moderately protrusive ears etc. Individually or in combination, these features only allow for a 'supe1iicial' comparison. The ability to discriminate between two people of similar general appearance is restricted. 

� 
The (relative) facial geometry� may or may not have been compared, but observation of identifiable landmarks is severely restricted by irnage quality. 

� 
Only one or two adequate images rnay be available for comparison. 


� An assessment of relative facial geometry requires that images of the crime perpetrator and the suspect are selected from the same viewpoints and scaled to be the same size. Alignment of the various landmarks (usually facial feature locations) observable, in each of the images, may reveal 
differences in the facial proportions or the sizes of the facial features between the two items of 
comparison. A comparison of facial geometry in this way is an assessment of the relative size, shape 
and position of the various facial features. Differences are more likely to be evident when the images 
are aligned on both horizontal and vertical axes rather than on one axis alone. 
DI LIGENCE 

Page 14 
19189V_PSNI I MJB 12 
Moderate support 
� The image quality permits a moderate amount of facial feature detail to be discerned: 
i.e. visible features can be described; tor example, the nose could be classified as having a narrow bridge and a straight ridge. 
� 
There are a few general facial features that can be compared. 

� 
Individually or in combination, the available imagery allows tor a reasonably robust comparison. The ability to discriminate between two people of similar appearance is restricted. 

� 
The (relative) facial geometry may be matching, but the ability to precisely locate landmarks is restricted and may therefore be somewhat imprecise. 

� 
The comparison may be limited in terms of the number of images available. 


Support 
� 
The image quality permits individual features to be compared in detail and a confident interpretation and comparison can be made. 

� 
There are some facial features that can be compared in detail. It is likely that the facial similariti(es/ditterences will be evident on multiple images. However, they may also be observed on only one occasion depending on image quality. The similarities may or may not be observed in corn bination. 

� 
Individually or in combination, the available imagery allows for a robust comparison. It should be possible to make a distinction between two people of broadly similar appearance. However, the ability to discriminate between two people bearing a close resemblance is restricted. 

� 
The (relative) facial geometry may be matching, but the ability to precisely locate landmarks is restricted and may therefore be somewhat imprecise. An assessment of geometry is likely to be limited to one or two instances i.e. from one or two differing viewpoints. 

� 
The comparison would usually be based on multiple images to provide consistency and confidence of interpretation. 


DI LIGE NCE 
\� �.�",'I �'ll,I',> 
'' ,l\ 0��1 
Page 15 19189v_PSNI /MJB /2 
Strong support 
� 
The image quality permits a range of features to be compared in detail and a very confident interpretation and comparison can be made. 

� 
There are some facial features that can be compared in detail. It is likely that the facial similarities/differences will be evident on multiple images. However, they may also be observed on only one occasion depending on image quality. The similarities may or may not be observed in combination. 

� 
Individually or in combination, the available imagery allows for a robust comparison. It should be possible to make a distinction between two people of similar appearance However, the ability to discriminate between two people bearing a very close resemblance is restricted. 

� 
The (relative) facial geometry may be matching; it is possible to confidently locate landmarks. A confident assessment of geometry is likely to be available from multiple viewpoints. 

� 
The comparison would usually be based on multiple images showing good detail to provide consistency and confidence of interpretation. 

� 
A lesse1� strength of comparison may be augmented by similarities/differences in tf1e location and size of tattoos, marks and scars, although fine detail of these features cannot be determined with confidence. 


Powerful support 
� 
The image quality permits a range of features to be compared in significant detail and an extremely confident interpretation and comparison can be made. 

� 
A significant number of facial features can be compared in detail. It is likely that these facial similarities/differences will be evident on multiple images. However, they may also be observed on only one occasion depending on image quality. The similarities may or may not be observed in combination. 

� 
Individually or in combination, the available imagery allows for a robust comparison. It should be possible to make a distinction between two people of very similar appearance. Two people would have to bear a striking resemblance in order for discrimination not to be possible. 

� 
Unless clearly justified by other aspects of the comparison, the assessment of (relative) facial geometry should be very robust, based on several landmarks that can be located with great confidence. A confident assessment of geometry is likely to be available from multiple viewpoints [notwithstanding comparisons based on high quality photography such as passports].

� 
The comparison would usually be based on multiple images showing good detail to provide consistency and confidence of interpretation. 

� 
A lesser strength of comparison may be augmented by similarities/differences in the location and size of tattoos, marks and scars, with the ability to compare some detail with reasonable confidence [shape or general delineation].
